SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Based on our field exploration, the Upper Access Road pavement generally 
consists of about 1 to 4 inches of AC placed over fill materials extending to depths of up 
to about 10 feet below the existing pavement surface. Beneath the fill materials, residual 
soils and saprolites were encountered in some of the borings extending to depths of about 
4 to 8 feet below the existing pavement surface. Alternating layers of basalt formation and 
clinker materials were encountered to the maximum depth explored of about 42 feet 
below the existing ground surface.  
Area RR-2 and Area RR-3 of Ridge Road generally were underlain by a pavement 
structure consisting of about 1 to 8 inches of AC over 3 to 17 inches of base materials 
and/or granular fill materials. Beneath the pavement structure, residual soils and 
saprolites were encountered extending to the maximum depth explored of about 11.5 feet 
below the existing ground surface. Area RR-1 was generally underlain by very stiff to hard 
residual soils extending to the maximum depth explored of about 11.5 feet below the 
existing ground surface.  
No groundwater was encountered in the borings at the time of the field 
investigation. However, groundwater levels are subject to change due to seasonal 
precipitation, surface water runoff, groundwater seepage, perched groundwater, and 
other factors. 
Based on our literature review, field observations, and DCP testing, it appears that 
Areas SS-1 and SS-2 are likely underlain by relatively stiff to hard residual and/or 
saprolitic soils extending to depths of about 3.5 to 17 feet below the existing ground 
surface. Based on our DCP testing along Upper Access Road, it appears that the existing 
ground surface on the downslope side of the Upper Access Road is likely underlain by 
relatively stiff to hard fill and/or residual soils to depths of about 0.5 to 10.8 feet below the 
existing ground surface.  
The type of erosion control and/or slope stabilization systems recommended for 
each section of Upper Access Road will depend on the existing lateral distance between 
the toe of the cut slope on the uphill side and the crest of the slope on the downhill side 
of the road. In sections with adequate lateral distance, a slope stabilization system, such 
as anchored wire mesh, would be appropriate. In areas without adequate lateral distance, 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page v 
Hawaii • California 
a retaining wall system retaining fill, such as a segmental retaining wall system, would be 
appropriate. 
Based on our field observations and the Government-provided information, 
Areas SS-1 and SS-2 appear to be remnants of previous slope failures. We recommend 
improving the drainage above this area and grading the existing landslide scars and the 
surrounding areas to have a maximum slope of 2H:1V. The prominent scarps should be 
cut and graded back at a 2H:1V slope to transition to the natural slope gradient of the 
slope above. To achieve the design slope gradient of 2H:1V within the scars, a 
combination of cut and fill may be considered. 
We understand the Government also would like to stabilize the existing eroded slope 
above the Area RR-1 water tanks. We also understand the Government does not desire to 
temporarily remove or relocate the existing security fence at the top of the eroded slope. 
Therefore, the inclination of the protected soil slope will be at about 1.5H:1V or steeper. Due 
to the relatively steep final slope condition and to reduce the amount of maintenance 
required to upkeep the protected slope, we recommend a slope protection system consisting 
of grouted rubble paving (GRP). 
Based on the Government-provided information, Areas LR-1, 3, and 5 contain 
potential rockfall hazards. Our site reconnaissance program identified numerous loose 
boulders and relatively tall near-vertical rock outcrops that could generate potential 
rockfall hazards. Based on our evaluation of the existing site conditions, we recommend 
a rockfall mitigation system consisting of slope clearing, scaling, installation of rockfall 
impact barriers, and installation of draped wire mesh and draped wire nets. 
For the project’s 35% Design Submittal, Geolabs identified potential rockfall 
hazards in Area 6. Based on our preliminary analyses, we believe there may be a low risk 
to human life and safety for workers driving through Area 6. Based on our evaluation of 
the existing site conditions, we recommend a rockfall mitigation system consisting of 
slope clearing, scaling, and installation of draped wire mesh and draped ring nets along 
the existing cut slope face. 
Our field exploration indicated that most of the project site may be underlain by 
medium hard to hard basalt rock formation at relatively shallow depths. Therefore, we 
anticipate that some difficult excavation conditions may arise during construction that will 
require the use of hoerams or chipping. It should be noted that laboratory compression 
testing of the basalt formation samples yielded uniaxial compressive strengths of up to 
about 24,000 psi. 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussion and specific 
design recommendations. 
END OF SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering exploration 
performed for the proposed Repair Roads and Erosion Control project (FY21 Special 
Project RM 17-1635) at Red Hill on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project location and 
general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
This report presents our geotechnical recommendations derived from our field 
exploration, laboratory testing, and engineering analyses for the proposed project. These 
recommendations are intended for the design of retaining structures, slope stabilization, 
rockfall mitigation systems, erosion control, earthwork, pavements, Low Impact 
Development (LID), and installation of utilities only. The findings and recommendations 
presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this report. 
1.1 
Project Considerations 
The project site is within the existing Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam (JBPHH) 
Red Hill Fuel Storage Facility at Red Hill on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. In general, the 
proposed work includes the design and construction of retaining walls, slope 
stabilizations, erosion control measures, access roadway repairs/reconstruction, storm 
water runoff controls, and rockfall hazard mitigation efforts at selected locations within the 
facility. The project will be conducted in two phases involving separate areas during the 
phased construction approach.  
Based on our site reconnaissance trips and field exploration, it appears that the 
facility roadways and slopes have experienced on-going deterioration and erosion, 
including slope and storm water runoff scour encroaching on the roadway, and 
appreciable deposition of eroded soils and rocks adjacent to the roadways. Existing 
power poles and overhead power lines were observed along the outside shoulder of 
Upper Access Road.  
Based on the information provided, we understand the project consists of the 
following phases and improvement areas. We understand that NAVFAC desires to 
combine “Phase 1” and “Phase 3” mentioned in the previous submittals. Therefore, the

1.2 
Purpose and Scope 
The purpose of our geotechnical engineering services on this project is to review 
the existing available subsurface information and collect additional subsurface 
information at the project site to develop geotechnical recommendations for evaluation, 
preliminary design, and final design of the retaining walls, slope surface stabilizations, 
erosion control measures, access roadway repairs/reconstruction, storm water runoff 
controls, and rockfall hazard mitigation efforts at selected locations within the project 
areas. To accomplish this, we propose an exploration program consisting of the following 
tasks and work efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of readily available information in the as-built files in 
the vicinity of the project site. 
2. 
Compile the available subsurface information and engineering properties of 
the subsurface geomaterials to perform our engineering analyses. 
3. 
Application for excavation permits from the applicable agencies and 
coordination of site access and underground utility toning by our project 
engineer. The Hawaii One-Call Center was notified following the field boring 
layout. 
4. 
Preparation and submittal of an accident prevention plan with activity 
hazard analysis in support of our field exploration activities on the roadways. 
5. 
Preparation and submittal of a traffic control plan in support of our field 
exploration activities on the roadways. 
6. 
Provision of a dedicated Site Safety and Health Officer (SSHO) during our 
field exploration work. 
7. 
Mobilization and demobilization of a truck-mounted drill rig, water truck, and 
two operators to the project site and back. 
8. 
Drilling and sampling of 36 exploratory borings extending to depths of about 
5 to 42 feet below the existing ground surface for a total of about 
899.6 linear feet of exploration.  
9. 
Provision of traffic control devices and signs during the geotechnical field 
exploration activities. 
10. 
Performance of field infiltration tests in six selected borings across the 
project site.

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
Of interest to our geotechnical engineering analysis are the surface and 
subsurface materials encountered at the project site, the engineering properties of the 
materials encountered, and the variability of the subsurface conditions across the project 
site. Therefore, the following subsections provide a description of the geologic setting of 
the project site, the surface and subsurface conditions encountered at the site, and a 
discussion on the items needed for seismic design, such as the seismicity, soil 
liquefaction, and the soil profile characteristics for seismic analysis. 
2.1 
General Geology 
The Island of O`ahu encompasses approximately 604 square miles of land area 
that may be divided into four major geographic provinces. The geographic provinces 
include the following: (1) Ko`olau Range, (2) Wai`anae Range, (3) Schofield Plateau, and 
(4) Coastal Plains. The majority of the Island of O`ahu was built by the extrusion of 
basaltic lavas from two primary shield volcanoes known as Wai`anae and Ko`olau. The 
older Wai`anae Volcano is estimated to be middle to late Pliocene in geochronologic age 
and forms the bulk of the western one-third of the island. The younger Ko`olau Volcano 
is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene (Ice Age) in age and forms the 
majority of the eastern two-thirds of the island. 
Wai`anae Volcano became extinct while Ko`olau Volcano remained active. 
Therefore, the westward flowing lava from the Ko`olau Volcano continued to bank against 
the weathered products of the Wai`anae Volcano to create the elevated area known as 
the Schofield Plateau located between the two volcanoes. 
Following the eruption of the bulk of the lavas, the coastal areas of O`ahu were 
partially submerged and emerged cyclically due to large-scale sea level fluctuations in 
response to periods of worldwide glaciation. In addition to the sea level fluctuations, the 
island mass was undergoing a gradual subsidence due to the island’s mass and isostatic 
adjustment of the earth’s crust beneath the island. As a result of the cyclic sea level 
fluctuations and the gradual subsidence of the island, the erosional baseline likewise 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii • California 
fluctuated, and major streams were drowned and buried and later emerged to renew their 
incision. 
The natural processes of erosion are closely related to the development and 
activation of the geologic hazard known as rockfall. Ground surface erosion may be 
defined as the process of land denudation by the wearing away of soil and rock by both 
physical and chemical processes. The processes may include the weathering of rock to 
form soils, the mass wasting of blocks of earth materials, and the erosive activity of 
streams, waves, wind, and underground water on the soil and rock materials. In Hawai`i, 
as well as other tropical regions, the processes of mass wasting combined with stream 
and ocean wave erosion are the most important forces acting to continually erode and 
stabilize landforms. Mass wasting may be defined as the downslope movement of a mass 
of rock and/or soil debris under the influence of gravity by means of events, such as 
rockfall, landslide, mudflow, soil avalanche, and soil creep. The driving force behind mass 
wasting is the force of gravity. 
The project site is underlain by basaltic rock representing the weathered products 
of the Ko`olau Volcanic Series lavas. The basalt formation is prominently layered with 
alternating beds of basalt clinker sandwiched between hard basalt rock beds that 
represent individual lava flows of both a`a and pāhoehoe type. As a result, the ground 
surface relief is somewhat irregular and “stepped” in character. The irregular hillslope 
surface is created by differential erosion between the weaker clinker layers and more 
indurated basalt rock layers representing the core of the lava flows. Therefore, periodic 
ledges of hard rock form greater relief in contrast to the more decomposed and low relief 
character of the bounding clinker layers. The geomorphic conditions described are 
conducive to erosion and potential rockfall conditions emanating from the upper natural 
slopes. In addition, the steep rock cut slopes adjacent to Lower Access Road and Upper 
Access Road are composed of alternating medium hard to very hard lava materials 
representing dense a`a lava core seams separated by weaker zones of cemented basaltic 
clinker (rocky rubble containing cobble and gravel size fragments welded by heat during 
deposition).

No known active geologic faults reside in the project area. Rockfall potential 
appears to be the primary geologic hazard that occurs at the project site. Other naturally 
induced potential hazards may include flash flood runoff from the steep rocky slopes and 
weakly defined drainage gullies during storm events. 
2.2 
General Site Conditions 
The project site is within the existing Joint Base Pearl Harbor-Hickam (JBPHH) 
Red Hill Fuel Storage Facility at Red Hill on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. The project area 
is generally bounded by the Halawa Industrial Area to the north and the Red Hill Island 
Palm Communities military housing to the south. A series of roads extend up the side of 
the mountain in a switchback fashion to provide access to the Red Hill Fuel Storage 
Facility complex. 
Based on previous studies conducted for the project area, we understand the lower 
unnamed access roads have been identified as Lower Access Road and Upper Access 
Road. Lower Access Road generally extends from the guard shack near the base of the 
mountain to the first switchback below Adit 5. Upper Access Road generally extends from 
this switchback to the gated entrance (and second switchback) leading to Icarus Way. 
The project also includes the section of Icarus Way extending from Upper Access Road 
to the gated entrance at the northeastern terminus of Icarus Way. Beyond the gated 
entrance, “Ridge Road” extends northeast to the eastern limit of the project. The project 
limits and roadways are shown on the Overall Site Plan, Plate 3. 
Based on our field observations, it appears that Lower Access Road and Upper 
Access Road were constructed by cutting into the existing mountain. An approximately 
22 to 47-foot tall near-vertical cut face exposing layers of basalt formation and volcanic 
clinker was observed along Lower Access Road near the guard shack. Within Area 3, 
Area 5, and Area 6 along Upper Access Road, the near-vertical cut height varies from 
approximately 14 to 42 feet. The existing slopes (Area LR-1) between Lower Access 
Road and Upper Access Road, and the existing slopes (Areas 3 and 5) between Upper 
Access Road and Icarus Way/Ridge Road are heavily vegetated. Ground slope 
inclinations of these slopes range from about two horizontal to one vertical (2H:1V) to 
near-vertical.

The landslide scar areas generally exposed reddish brown residual and saprolitic soils. 
Based on our field observations, it appeared that most of the landslide material had 
traveled further downslope, likely due to subsequent storm water runoff. The areas 
around the landslide scars were heavily vegetated. However, the scar areas contained 
minimal vegetative growth.  
A bare, eroded soil slope was observed above the existing water tanks between 
Areas RR-1 and RR-2. Based on the available topographic information, the slope is 
approximately 15 feet tall with a slope face inclination of about 1.5H:1V to 1H:1V. Based 
on our field observations, the slope generally exposes reddish brown residual and 
saprolitic soils. Eroded drainage gullies on the slope face suggest that storm water runoff 
overtops the slope and flows towards the existing water tanks.  
An existing unpaved, unnamed, gravel access road connects Upper Access Road 
to Icarus Way. Based on the available topographic information, we understand that the 
existing access road is approximately 7 to 22 feet wide with slope inclinations of up to 
about 20 percent. Our field observations noted that much of the gravel on the access road 
had eroded away, and drainage gullies had been eroded into the existing subgrade. The 
subgrade generally consisted of reddish brown residual soil. Remnant asphaltic concrete 
pavement was noted near the bottom of the access road.

2.3 
Site Reconnaissance 
Site reconnaissance of the existing slopes (Area LR-1) between Lower Access 
Road and Upper Access Road, and the existing slopes (Area 3 and Area 5) between 
Upper Access Road and Ridge Road were performed by our project engineers on 
September 30, November 2, and November 3, 2020 to evaluate the existing slope 
conditions. An additional site reconnaissance was conducted on February 8, 2021 to 
evaluate the additive areas: Area 6, the eroded slope above the existing Area RR-1 water 
tanks, and the gravel access road between Upper Access Road and Icarus Way. Our 
general observations of the eroded slope above the existing water tanks and the gravel 
access road are presented in the previous section. The following sections present our 
observations of the Area LR-1, Area 3, and Area 5 slope areas and the cut slope face in 
Area 6. The location of slope features, such as labeled boulders and boulder fields, are 
presented on the Site Plans (Plates 4.1 through 4.4). Site Reconnaissance Photographs 
depicting the existing site conditions of the areas observed are presented on Plates 7.1 
through 7.27. 
2.3.1 Area LR-1 Slope 
Geolabs previously performed site reconnaissance and provided rockfall mitigation 
recommendations for a portion of Area LR-1. Details of the site reconnaissance 
observations and geotechnical recommendations are presented in the Basis of 
Design Report for MCON Project P-058 (Geolabs, 2018). Therefore, our recent 
site reconnaissance program focused on the slope areas to the east and west of 
the existing rockfall impact barrier in Area LR-1. 
In general, the existing slope surface was mildly to heavily vegetated with haole 
koa shrubbery, grasses, and some large trees. The face of the slope generally 
consisted of soil material. However, frequent rock outcrops and natural, near-
vertical rock faces were observed.  
An appreciable quantity of construction debris (e.g. asphaltic concrete pavement 
pieces and manmade debris) and loose basaltic cobbles and boulders were 
observed in the upper portion of the slope. The basaltic cobbles and boulders 
appear to be overcast material, possibly generated from the construction of Upper

blocky basalt formation. Clinker seams were frequently observed, especially along 
the faces of the taller rock outcrops. In general, we did not observe any immediate 
rockfall hazards on the rock outcrop faces, such as loose blocks or precarious 
overhanging material. However, there is a potential for rockfall material to emanate 
from the outcrops due to future erosion and/or the growth of vegetation on or near 
the faces of the rock outcrops. Banyan trees were observed growing near a few of 
the rock outcrops. The root systems of the trees generally appeared to stabilize 
the rock outcrop faces. The locations of the Rock Outcrops observed also are

2.3.2 Area 3 and Area 5 Slope 
The existing slopes at Area 3 and Area 5 were observed to be moderately to 
heavily vegetated. Haole koa shrubbery, grasses, and some tall palm trees were 
observed. The thickness of the vegetation prevented access to many areas on the 
lower portion of the slope and obscured visual observation of the slope surface, 
potential rockfall hazards, and the faces of the existing near-vertical rock outcrops.

Boulder 11 appeared to be formed from the weathering of a nearby blocky rock 
outcrop.

order of about 4 to 15 feet in vertical height. However, the density of the vegetation 
prevented us from observing sections of the exposed rock outcrops and prevented 
access in certain areas. Based on the topographic information provided, we 
believe there are other near-vertical rock outcrops about 20 to 30 feet in height 
near the base of the existing slope. In general, the rock outcrops exposed blocky 
basalt formation. We did not observe any immediate rockfall hazards on the rock 
outcrop faces, such as loose blocks or precarious overhanging material. However, 
there is a potential for rockfall material to emanate from the outcrops due to future 
erosion and/or the growth of vegetation on or near the faces of the rock outcrops.

We believe that the most likely potential rockfall hazards within Area 3 and Area 5

2.3.3 Area 6 
The existing cut slope along Upper Access Road generally exposed blocky basalt 
formation with weathered clinker seams. The near-vertical rock face was relatively 
free of vegetation except for koa haole trees near the top of the slope and one 
relatively large banyan tree with roots extending over the slope face. Numerous 
cobble and boulder-sized rocks (approximately 1 to 8 feet in largest diameter) were 
observed in the unpaved shoulder area at the base of the slope. Based on our field 
observations, it was unclear which cobbles/boulders were deposited naturally, 
were the result of previous construction activity, or were pushed to the shoulder 
area by the Red Hill Facility workers. However, based on the conditions of the cut 
rock face and our previous reconnaissance on the upper slope, we believe that the 
source of this rock material is the cut rock face, and not from a rock source further 
upslope.  
In general, the size of the rock blocks on the exposed cut face were larger on the 
western half of Area 6. The fracture pattern of the rock formation formed smaller 
rock blocks on the eastern half of Area 6 and continued through Areas 3 and 5. 
Therefore, the size of the potential rockfall hazards is generally larger in the 
western half of Area 6 (exceeding 30 inches in largest dimension). Several 
potential rockfall hazards were observed on the cut face. These included rock 
blocks with relatively wide fractures and minimal basal support. In addition, 
weathering of the clinker seams could create future potential rockfall hazards. 
However, precarious overhanging blocks were not observed at the time of our field 
reconnaissance.

and Manana silty clay loam (MoC). The land type (rRK) is made up of exposed or near-

extending to depths of about 5 to 42 feet below the existing ground surface. We also

from the existing ground surface (unpaved shoulder area) to about 10 feet below the 
existing ground surface. The basalt formation and clinker were encountered to the 
maximum depth explored of about 42 feet below the existing ground surface. The basalt 
formation generally graded to moderately fractured, slightly weathered, and hard to very 
hard. Localized areas of severe fracturing and moderate weathering were encountered, 
as well as massive, unweathered sections of basalt formation. Both cemented and 
uncemented layers of volcanic clinker were encountered between the layers of basalt 
formation. The cemented clinker generally graded to moderately fractured, moderately 
weathered, and medium hard with localized pockets of soft, highly weathered material.

be noted that a poorly backfilled void was encountered from about 4.5 to 9.5 feet below 
the existing ground surface in Boring No. 11 (within the central portion of the Upper 
Access Road alignment).

2.6 
Seismic Design Considerations 
The project site will be subjected to seismic activity and should be evaluated for 
potential soil liquefaction. Seismic design of the proposed project will be based on the 
Unified Facilities Criteria “Seismic Design of Buildings” (UFC 3-301-01 dated October 1, 
2019), which references the 2018 International Building Code (IBC 2018) and American 
Society of Civil Engineers (ASCE) 7-16, Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other 
Structures, 2016 Edition. 
2.6.1 Earthquakes and Seismicity 
In general, earthquakes throughout the world are caused by shifts in the tectonic 
plates. In contrast, earthquake activity in Hawaii is linked primarily to volcanic 
activity; therefore, earthquake activity in Hawaii generally occurs before or during 
volcanic eruptions. In addition, earthquakes may result from the underground 
movement of magma that comes close to the surface but does not erupt. The 
Island of Hawaii experiences thousands of earthquakes each year, but most are 
so small that only sensitive instruments can detect them. However, some of the 
earthquakes are strong enough to be felt, and a few cause minor to moderate 
damage. 
In general, earthquakes associated with volcanic activity are most common on the 
Island of Hawaii. Earthquakes that are directly associated with the movement of 
magma are concentrated beneath the active Kilauea and Mauna Loa Volcanoes 
on the Island of Hawaii. Because most earthquakes in Hawaii (over 90 percent) 
are related to volcanic activity, the risk of seismic activity and degree of ground 
shaking diminishes with increased distance from the Island of Hawaii. The Island 
of Hawaii has experienced numerous earthquakes greater than Magnitude 5 
(M5+); however, earthquakes are not confined only to the Island of Hawaii. 
To a lesser degree, the Island of Maui has experienced several earthquakes 
greater than Magnitude 5. Therefore, moderate to strong earthquakes have 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii • California 
occurred in the County of Maui. The effects of earthquakes occurring on the 
Islands of Hawaii and Maui may be felt on the Island of Oahu. For example, small 
landslides occurred on the Island of Oahu as a result of the Maui Earthquake of 
1938 (M6.8). Some houses on the Island of Oahu were reportedly damaged 
because of the Lanai Earthquake of 1871 (M7+). 
In the last 150 years of recorded history, we are not aware of earthquakes greater 
than Magnitude 6 that have occurred on the Island of Oahu. An earthquake of 
Magnitude 4.8 to 5.0 occurred along the Diamond Head Fault in 1948 on the Island 
of Oahu. The moderate tremor resulted in broken store windows, ruptured building 
walls, and broken underground water mains. 
2.6.2 Liquefaction Potential 
Based on the International Building Code (2018 Edition), the project site may be 
subjected to seismic activity and should be evaluated for potential soil liquefaction. 
Soil liquefaction is a condition where saturated cohesionless soils near the ground 
surface undergo a substantial loss of strength due to the build-up of excess pore 
water pressures resulting from cyclic stress applications induced by earthquakes. 
In this process, when the loose saturated sand deposit is subjected to vibration 
(such as during an earthquake), the soil tends to densify and decrease in volume 
causing an increase in pore water pressure. If drainage is unable to occur rapidly 
enough to dissipate the build-up of pore water pressure, the effective stress 
(internal strength) of the soil is reduced. Under sustained vibrations, the pore water 
pressure build-up could equal the overburden pressure, essentially reducing the 
soil shear strength to zero and causing it to behave as a viscous fluid. During 
liquefaction, the soil acquires sufficient mobility to permit both horizontal and 
vertical movements, and if not confined, will result in significant deformations. 
Soils most susceptible to liquefaction are loose, uniformly graded, fine-grained 
sands and loose silts with little cohesion. The major factors affecting the 
liquefaction characteristics of a soil deposit are as follows: 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii • California 
FACTORS 
LIQUEFACTION SUSCEPTIBILITY 
Grain Size Distribution 
Fine and uniform sands and silts are 
more susceptible to liquefaction than 
coarse or well-graded sands. 
Initial Relative Density 
Loose sands and silts are most 
susceptible to liquefaction.  Liquefaction 
potential is inversely proportional to 
relative density. 
Magnitude and Duration of Vibration 
Liquefaction potential is directly 
proportional to the magnitude and 
duration of the earthquake. 
In general, the subsurface information obtained from the drilled borings indicate 
that the project site is underlain by relatively stiff/dense residual and saprolitic soils 
overlying alternating layers of basalt formation and clinker. Based on the 
subsurface conditions encountered in our field exploration, the geology of the area, 
and our engineering analyses, the potential for soil liquefaction at the project site 
is non-existent due to the presence of relatively stiff/dense residual and saprolitic 
soils, basalt formation with clinker seams, and the absence of loose granular soils 
below the groundwater table within the depths explored. Therefore, the potential 
for liquefaction is not a design consideration for this project site. 
2.6.3 Site Classification 
Based on the subsurface materials encountered and the geologic setting of the 
area, we believe that the project site may be classified as a “Very Dense Soil and 
Soft Rock” site corresponding to a Site Class “C” soil profile in accordance with 
Table 20.3-1 (Site Classification) of ASCE 7-16 (Minimum Design Loads for 
Buildings and Other Structures) referenced by the International Building Code, 
2018 Edition. Based on Site Class C, the following seismic design parameters 
were estimated and may be used for seismic analysis of this project.

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
Based on our field exploration, the Upper Access Road pavement generally 
consists of about 1 to 4 inches of AC over fill material, residual and saprolitic soils, and 
alternating layers of basalt formation and clinker extending to the maximum depth 
explored of about 42 feet below the existing ground surface. Areas RR-2 and RR-3 
generally were underlain by a pavement structure consisting of about 1 to 8 inches of AC 
over 3 to 17 inches of base and/or fill material and residual/saprolitic soils extending to 
the maximum depth explored of about 11.5 feet below the existing ground surface. 
Area RR-1 generally was underlain by residual soils extending to the maximum depth 
explored of about 11.5 feet below the existing ground surface. Groundwater was not 
encountered in the borings at the time of the field investigation. 
Based on our literature review, field observations, and DCP testing, it appears that 
Areas SS-1 and SS-2 likely are underlain by relatively stiff to hard residual and/or 
saprolitic soils extending to depths of about 3.5 to 17 feet below the existing ground 
surface. Based on our DCP testing along Upper Access Road, it appears that the existing 
ground surface on the downslope side of Upper Access Road likely is underlain by 
relatively stiff to hard fill and/or residual soils to depths of about 0.5 to 10.8 feet below the 
existing ground surface.  
The type of erosion control and/or slope stabilization systems recommended for 
each section of Upper Access Road will depend on the existing lateral distance between 
the toe of the cut slope on the uphill side and the crest of the slope on the downhill side 
of the road. In sections with adequate distance, a slope stabilization system, such as 
anchored wire mesh (AWM), would be appropriate. In areas without adequate distance, 
a retaining wall system retaining a fill condition, such as a segmental retaining wall, would 
be appropriate. 
We recommend grading the existing landslide scars at Areas SS-1 and SS-2 and 
the surrounding areas to have a slope inclination of 2H:1V or flatter. The prominent scarps 
should be cut and graded back at a 2H:1V slope to transition to the natural slope gradient 
of the slope above.

Based on the Government-provided information, Area LR-1, Area 3, and Area 5 
contain potential rockfall hazards. Based on our evaluation of the existing site conditions, 
we recommend a rockfall mitigation system consisting of slope clearing, scaling, 
installation of rockfall impact barriers, and installation of draped wire mesh and draped 
ring nets below the barrier.  
For the project’s 35% Design Submittal, Geolabs identified potential rockfall 
hazards in Area 6. Based on our analyses, we believe that there may be a low risk to 
human life and safety for workers driving through Area 6. Based on our evaluation of the 
existing site conditions, we recommend a rockfall mitigation system consisting of slope 
clearing, scaling, and installation of draped wire mesh and draped ring nets along the 
existing cut slope face. 
Detailed discussions of our geotechnical recommendations for design of the 
project are presented in the following sections of this report. 
3.1 
Upper Access Road Improvements 
Based on the information provided from the Government and the design team’s 
field observations, the downslope side of Upper Access Road is experiencing appreciable 
erosion where surface water appears to concentrate. To mitigate further erosion at these 
locations, drainage improvements will be provided adjacent to Upper Access Road. In 
addition, the access road will be reconstructed and widened to a two-lane, 16-foot wide, 
paved roadway.  
The type of erosion control and/or slope stabilization systems recommended for 
each section of Upper Access Road will depend on the severity of the erosion and the 
existing lateral distance between the toe of the cut slope on the uphill side and the crest 
of the slope on the downhill side of the road. In sections of moderate erosion and where 
adequate distance is available to construct the widened roadway and drainage 
improvements, a slope stabilization system, such as anchored wire mesh (AWM), would 
be appropriate and may be considered. In areas with more severe erosion and without 
adequate space to construct the widened Upper Access Road and drainage 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 23 
Hawaii • California 
improvements, a retaining wall system retaining a fill condition, such as a segmental 
retaining wall, would be appropriate. 
As previously noted, a poorly-backfilled void was encountered from about 4.5 to 
9.5 feet below the existing ground surface in Boring No. 11 within the central portion of 
the Upper Access Road alignment. Therefore, the pavement subgrade in this area should 
be proof-rolled prior to installing the AWM system to avoid excessive grout loss during 
anchor installation. The pavement subgrade should be proof-rolled with a vibratory drum 
roller (minimum 10 tons static weight) a minimum of eight passes to help detect and 
collapse near-surface cavities and/or voids. 
The vibratory drum roller or heavy equipment should be operated at a speed of 
about 300 feet per minute. Yielding areas, loose areas, or cavities disclosed during 
clearing and proof-rolling operations should be over-excavated and backfilled with 
compacted fill materials followed by the placement of a geogrid layer (Tensar TX-7, 
BX1200, or equivalent) over the pavement subgrade prior to the placement of the 
aggregate base course and flexible pavement surface course to help reinforce the 
pavement subgrade for the new pavements in this area. 
3.2 
Anchored Wire Mesh 
The anchored wire mesh system is a slope treatment that involves installation of 
high-strength steel wire mesh panels anchored onto the slope face with grouted soil/rock 
anchors in an effort to prevent the soils and rocks from moving or falling, thus providing 
stability. The primary intent of this stabilization system is to closely pin the wire mesh to 
the slope face as a retention system.  
The slope to be anchored with steel wire mesh must be trimmed of vegetation and 
scaled of loose rocks prior to the installation of the anchored wire mesh system. Detailed 
descriptions of the individual components of the anchored wire mesh system are 
presented below: 
3.2.1 High-Strength Wire Mesh 
The wire mesh should be alloyed high-strength carbon steel wire with a minimum 
tensile strength of 256,000 pounds per square inch (psi). The wire mesh should be 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 24 
Hawaii • California 
woven construction, diamond shaped, and made with 4-mm (0.157-inch) diameter 
wire. The ends of each wire should be formed into a loop and should be twisted. 
The loops of the wire mesh should be fastened together to prevent unraveling of 
the mesh. The wire should be galvanized with a zinc/aluminum coating with a 
minimum weight of 0.40 ounces per square foot (120 grams per square meter) for 
Level 3 drawn Zn5-Al wire in accordance with ASTM A1007.  
3.2.2 Press Claws (Compression Claws) 
The press claws (formerly known as compression claws) should be 6-mm 
(0.236-inch) diameter carbon steel bars and hot-dipped galvanized with a minimum 
layer thickness of 85 microns. The smaller (standard) press claws should be used 
to fasten the mesh panels together, and the bigger (special) press claws should 
be used to fasten the mesh panels to the boundary ropes. 
3.2.3 Spike Plates 
The spike plates should be made from 7-mm (0.28-inch) thick steel and should be 
hot dipped galvanized with a minimum layer thickness of 85 microns. The spike 
plate should be diamond shaped with a width of 205 mm (8.07 inches) and a length 
of 330 mm (13 inches). 
3.2.4 Boundary Ropes 
Boundary ropes should have a diameter of 12 mm (0.5 inches) and should be PVC 
coated. The rope should consist of 6 by 19 construction, IWRC and galvanized 
with a minimum breaking strength of 23,940 pounds. The rope should meet 
Federal Specification RR-W-410D or equivalent including galvanizing. 
3.2.5 Grouted Soil/Rock Anchors 
The grouted soil/rock anchors should consist of 1.375-inch diameter solid threaded 
bar (Grade 75).  
The anchored wire mesh system and all associated hardware, such as the high-
strength steel wire mesh, press claws, and spike plates should be powder coated with a 
black pigment or another color as directed by the owner. Boundary wire ropes should be 
PVC coated in a black matte finish unless directed otherwise by the owner. All other 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 25 
Hawaii • California 
exposed steel elements should be painted with a black colored rubberized paint. The 
following provides a brief outline of the anchored wire mesh installation procedures. 
1. 
Trim slope face of vegetation, loose materials, and protruding rock outcrops 
to produce a smooth surface. Highly irregular surfaces would not be 
acceptable. 
2. 
Stakeout grouted soil/rock anchors in general accordance with the project 
spacing requirements considering the low spots on the slope. 
3. 
Excavate the dell for pre-tensioning of the anchored wire mesh before 
drilling the grouted soil/rock anchors. 
4. 
Install the grouted soil/rock anchors (anchor heads should not project above 
the terrain line or predominant slope line). 
5. 
Lay the erosion control matting on the slope. 
6. 
Lay the steel wire mesh panels on the slope over the erosion control 
matting. 
7. 
Join or seam the steel wire mesh panels together. 
8. 
Fit the boundary wire ropes around the perimeter of the anchored wire mesh 
system. 
9. 
Install the spike plates and pre-tension with a torque wrench or hydraulic 
press to the specified forces. 
10. 
Hydromulch all exposed cut slope areas after mesh installation. 
Based on our evaluation for the slope, we recommend that the grouted soil/rock 
anchor be a minimum of 20 feet long, spaced at 8-foot on-center in a staggered 
pattern. The drilled hole for the grouted soil/rock anchor should be a minimum of 
6 inches in diameter. The grout should be non-shrink grout and should have a 
minimum unconfined compressive strength of 5,000 psi or greater. The grouted 
soil/rock anchors should have an ultimate pullout capacity of 60 kips. 
The grouted soil/rock anchors should be set in low spots, where possible, to pull 
the anchored wire mesh into the depressions and against the ground. This will 
avoid major hollow spaces and reduce the quantity of vegetation material required 
during greening of the slope face. If the anchor pattern does not permit setting 
grouted soil/rock anchors in the low spots, supplemental (short) anchors may be 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 26 
Hawaii • California 
installed to achieve this purpose. The supplemental grouted soil/rock anchors 
should consist of 1.375-inch diameter solid threaded bar (grade 75) with a 
minimum embedment length of 8 feet. 
Due to Hawaii`s climate, which is moderately to highly corrosive, the useful life of 
the anchored wire mesh system could be highly variable, depending on the quality 
of the steel wire mesh and hardware, corrosion protection, and workmanship 
employed during the anchored wire mesh installation. 
A minimum of 10 percent of the grouted soil/rock anchors should be proof tested. 
Proof tests should be performed by incrementally loading the anchor to a maximum 
test load of 150 percent of the design pullout capacity. An electronic load cell 
should be used to measure the applied load to the anchor. The anchor head 
movement during the test should be measured and recorded. 
3.3 
Segmental Retaining Walls 
In general, the segmental retaining wall system is a composite wall system utilizing 
high-density polyethylene, or other reinforcing elements, to reinforce the backfill zone and 
improve the shear strength of the reinforced soil zone. This composite system essentially 
forms a gravity wall structure with an ability to tolerate significant total and differential 
settlements. In addition, segmental retaining walls are also desirable due to the flexibility 
of the wall, ease of construction, high load carrying capacity, and economy. 
Design of the segmental retaining wall system will need to take into consideration 
both the external and internal stability of the structure. In evaluating external stability, the 
retaining wall must satisfy four stability conditions: (1) bearing failure, (2) translational 
sliding, (3) overturning stability, and (4) overall slope stability. Geotechnical design 
parameters to evaluate these stability conditions are presented in the following 
subsections. Some of the geotechnical parameters necessary in evaluating the internal 
stability of the retaining wall are presented in the "Reinforced Fill and Backfill Materials" 
subsection.

designed in accordance with the following geotechnical parameters.

The base of wall (reinforced soil mass) should maintain a minimum 6-foot setback 
distance measured horizontally from the outside edge of the footing to the face of 
the slope for sloping ground conditions. The contribution to passive pressure 
resistance due to the wedge of soil on a sloping ground condition should be 
neglected. 
The wall subgrades should be compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative 
compaction to provide a firm an unyielding base. Soft and/or loose soils 
encountered at the wall subgrades should be over-excavated to a minimum depth 
of 2 feet below the bottom of wall elevation. The over-excavation also should 
extend a minimum of 2 feet laterally beyond the front face of the walls. The 
resulting over-excavation may be backfilled with compacted on-site soils (minimum 
95 percent relative compaction).

The retained soil behind the segmental retaining wall structure will exert lateral 
earth pressures on the structure. The recommended static lateral earth pressures 
for design of retaining walls, expressed in equivalent fluid pressures of pounds per 
square foot per foot of depth (pcf), are presented in the following table. These 
lateral earth pressures do not include hydrostatic pressures that might be caused 
by groundwater trapped behind the structures.

The values provided above assume that the on-site soils or select granular fill 
materials will be used to backfill behind the walls. We recommend compacting the 
backfill behind the retaining structures to between 90 and 95 percent relative 
compaction. Over-compaction of the retaining structure backfill should be avoided. 
In general, an active condition may be used for gravity retaining walls and walls 
that are free to deflect by as much as 0.5 percent of the wall height. If the tops of 
the walls are not free to deflect beyond this degree, or are restrained, the walls 
should be designed for the at-rest condition. 
Surcharge stresses due to areal surcharges, traffic loads, line loads, and point 
loads within a horizontal distance equal to the depth of the wall should be 
considered in the design. For uniform surcharge stresses imposed on the loaded 
side of the retaining structure, a rectangular distribution with a uniform pressure 
equal to 47 percent of the vertical surcharge pressure acting on the entire height 
of the structure, which is restrained, may be used in the design. For retaining 
structures that are free to deflect (cantilever), a rectangular distribution equal to 
31 percent of the vertical surcharge pressure acting over the entire height of the 
structure may be used for the design. Additional analyses during design may be 
needed to evaluate the surcharge effects of point loads and line loads. 
Dynamic lateral earth forces due to seismic loading (a = 0.318g) may be estimated 
by using 6H2 pounds per linear foot of wall length for level backfill conditions, where 
H is the height of the wall in feet. It should be noted that the dynamic lateral earth 
forces provided assume that the wall will be allowed to move laterally by up to

SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 29 
Hawaii • California 
about 1 to 2 inches in the event of an earthquake. For sloping ground conditions 
with a maximum slope inclination of 3H:1V, the dynamic lateral earth forces due to 
seismic loading may be estimated by using 12H2 pounds per linear foot of wall 
length. The resultant force should be assumed to act through the mid-height of the 
wall. The dynamic lateral earth forces are in addition to the static lateral earth 
pressures provided above. An appropriately reduced factor of safety may be used 
when dynamic lateral earth forces are accounted for in the design of the retaining 
structures. 
In general, a subdrain is recommended behind the segmental retaining wall 
structure to collect and discharge excess water that may infiltrate behind the wall 
structure. A typical subdrain would consist of a perforated pipe (with perforations 
down) enclosed by at least 12 inches of permeable drainage material, such as 
open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation). The perforated pipe should be 
directed to discharge into a proper drainage facility. The permeable drainage 
material should be wrapped in a non-woven filter fabric, such as Mirafi 180N or 
equivalent. Unless covered by concrete slabs, the upper 12 inches of backfill 
should consist of relatively impervious material (compacted on-site soils) to reduce 
the potential for significant water infiltration behind the retaining wall. 
3.3.3 Overall Slope Stability 
A minimum factor of safety of 1.5 is recommended for the short-term and long-term 
static stability of the segmental retaining walls. In addition, we recommend 
providing a minimum base width to wall height ratio of at least 0.70 for the 
segmental retaining wall structures with level backfill conditions planned at the 
project site. For segmental retaining walls with a backslope inclination of 3H:1V or 
flatter, a minimum base width to wall height ratio of at least 0.90 is recommended. 
3.3.4 Reinforced Fill and Backfill Materials 
Considering the relative height of the segmental retaining walls proposed, we 
recommend utilizing granular fill materials for the reinforced fill materials. In 
general, the imported select granular fill materials should be well graded from 
coarse to fine with particles no larger than 3 inches in largest dimension. The 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC.

Hawaii • California 
material also should contain 15 percent or less particles passing the No. 200 sieve. 
The material should have a California Bearing Ratio (CBR) value of 25 or higher 
and a swell potential of 1 percent or less when tested in accordance with 
ASTM D1883. As an alternative to select granular fill, aggregate subbase course 
may be used.  
In addition, the reinforced fill material (imported select granular fill materials or 
aggregate subbase course) should have an angle of internal friction of at least 
36 degrees when tested by the standard direct shear test (ASTM D3080). The 
sample to be tested should be compacted to 95 percent relative compaction at 
moisture contents above the optimum. 
Reinforced fill materials should be placed in level loose lifts not exceeding 8 inches 
in loose thickness and be compacted to at least 95 percent of the maximum dry 
density established in accordance with ASTM D1557 at moisture contents above 
the optimum.

We understand a conventional retaining wall is planned on the downslope side of

3.5 
Area 4 – Overhanging Rock Mass 
We understand there is an undercut rock mass next to the entrance of an existing 
dynamite storage magazine along Upper Access Road between Adit 4 and Adit 5. 
Portions of the overhanging rock have been grouted in place. The primary mechanism for 
rockfall activity at Area 4 is the possible weathering, deterioration, and expansion of rock 
fractures within the overhanging rock material with the passage of time. Therefore, we 
recommend installing shotcrete or cast-in-place concrete support pedestals within the 
existing undercut void to provide basal support for the overhanging material. 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 31 
Hawaii • California 
At the onset of construction, we recommend cutting and removing the existing 
vegetation within the identified undercut void and surrounding the rock outcropping. In 
addition, loose debris, deleterious material, and other unsuitable or yielding materials 
should be removed from the undercut voids and properly disposed of before commencing 
with the shotcrete or cast-in-place concrete pedestal installation work. 
The support pedestal should consist of a fiber-reinforced shotcrete or a 
fiber-reinforced cast-in-place concrete mass that will completely infill the undercut void 
area. To secure the pedestal in place within the void, we recommend providing 1-inch 
diameter fiber-reinforced polymer (FRP) grouted anchors with centralizers. The anchors 
should be spaced 24-inches on-center, each way, along the top and bottom of the void. 
The grouted anchors should be embedded a minimum of 12 inches in competent/basalt 
material and protrude a minimum of 12 inches into the shotcrete/concrete. 
The shotcrete pedestal should be well-drained to reduce the potential build-up of 
hydrostatic pressures. A typical drainage system would consist of 2-foot wide strips of a 
prefabricated drainage product (such as Miradrain/Enkadrain or equivalent), placed on 
the back vertical face of the undercut void at a maximum horizontal spacing of 5 feet 
on-center. The prefabricated drainage product should extend from the top to the base of 
the undercut void. The drainage strips should be hydraulically connected to a drainage 
system consisting of permeable backfill material, such as open-graded gravel (ASTM 
C33, No. 67 gradation), wrapped with non-woven filter fabric (Mirafi 180N or equal) placed 
up against the back base of the undercut void. The wrapped permeable backfill should 
be hydraulically connected to a weep hole that daylights at the front of the pedestal.  
Consideration should be given to sculpting and staining the outer face of the 
shotcrete or cast-in-place concrete pedestal to match the characteristics of the 
surrounding basalt rock.

3.7 
Areas SS-1 and SS-2 
Based on our field observations and the Government-provided information, 
Areas SS-1 and SS-2 appear to be remnants of previous slope failures. At the time of our 
field observations, the areas within the landslide scars exposed residual soil and/or 
weathered basalt rock. Subsequent rain events have carved relatively deep drainage 
paths within the exposed soil in Area SS-1. Both areas exhibit a prominent scarp at the 
head of the exposed areas.

We recommend grading the existing landslide scars and the surrounding areas to 
have a slope inclination of 2H:1V or flatter. The prominent scarps should be cut and 
graded back at a 2H:1V slope or flatter to transition to the natural slope gradient of the 
slope above. To achieve the design slope gradient of 2H:1V within the scars, a 
combination of cut and fill may be used. For slopes with vertical heights greater than 
30 feet, we recommend providing a minimum 8-foot wide bench at the midpoint of the 
slope or at maximum 30-foot vertical height intervals to reduce the potential for significant 
erosion due to surface water runoff. 
After grading, erosion control matting (such as North American Green RollMax 
Vmax C350 or equivalent) should be installed on the exposed soil areas. The erosion 
control matting should be pinned to the slope face per the manufacturer’s 
recommendations. Hydromulch should then be applied to the erosion control matting to 
promote the growth of vegetation as quickly as possible.

consist of drainage improvements. In addition, we understand the unpaved access road 
within Area RR-1 will be regraded and surfaced with compacted gravel. Detention basins 
are planned along Ridge Road for storm water collection and disposal.

It should be noted that there is a potential for raveling and rutting of the compacted

the gravel-surfaced road. We believe most of the maintenance will consist of 
periodic grading to remove ruts and depressions caused by the environment and

The subgrade soils under the new pavement structural section should be scarified 
to a minimum depth of 8 inches, moisture-conditioned to at least 2 percent above 
the optimum moisture content, and compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative 
compaction. Relative compaction refers to the in-place dry density of soil 
expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil 
established in accordance with ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water 
content (percentage by dry weight) corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
Aggregate base course should have a laboratory CBR value of 80 or more and 
meet the material requirements as specified in UFC 3-250-01, Pavement Design 
For Roads and Parking Areas. Imported materials should be tested for 
conformance with these recommendations prior to delivery to the project site for 
the intended use.

pavement subgrade preparation to observe whether undesirable materials are 
encountered during the excavation and scarification process and to confirm 
whether the exposed soil conditions are similar to those encountered in the 
previous field exploration performed by others. California Bearing Ratio (CBR) 
tests and/or field observations should be performed on the actual subgrade soils 
during construction to confirm that the above design section is adequate.

the test depth, a 4-inch diameter solid PVC casing was set to the bottom of the 
drilled hole to allow infiltration only through the soil exposed on the bottom of the 
boring.

the underlying subsurface materials. Each test consisted of four trials of filling the 
casing with 24 inches of water and taking periodic readings over a one-hour trial 
period. The infiltration rate is calculated based on the result of the fourth and the 
last trial for each test location. The results are summarized in the table below. 
Detailed information of the tests is presented in Appendix E.

3.9 
Additive Item – Eroded Slope Above RR-1 Water Tanks 
Based on our field observations and the available topographic information, the slope 
is approximately 15 feet tall with a slope face inclination of about 1.5H:1V to 1H:1V. Based 
on our field observations, the slope generally exposes reddish brown residual and saprolitic 
soils. We understand the Government does not want to temporarily remove or relocate the 
existing security fence at the top of the eroded slope. Therefore, the final grade of the 
protected soil slope will remain at about 1.5H:1V or steeper.  
Due to the relatively steep final slope condition and to reduce the amount of 
maintenance required to upkeep the slope, we recommend a slope protection system 
consisting of grouted rubble paving (GRP). The GRP slope protection should be a minimum 
of 18 inches thick and should be keyed into the existing slope (minimum 3 feet deep by 
2 feet wide) to reduce undermining of the GRP system due to erosion. 
At the on-set of earthwork, the area within the contract grading limits at the slope 
failure area should be thoroughly cleared and grubbed. Vegetation, debris, deleterious 
materials, and other unsuitable materials should be removed and disposed of properly off-
site or in a designated area to reduce the potential for contamination of the excavated 
materials. 
The GRP slope protection system should be well drained to reduce the potential for 
build-up of hydrostatic pressures. A typical drainage system would consist of 2-foot wide 
strips of a prefabricated drainage composite product placed on the face of the slope and 
failure zone. The prefabricated drainage composite product should extend from the top of 
the slope to the base of the slope and should be hydraulically connected to a weep hole at 
the base of the wall. The weepholes should be spaced no further than 6 feet apart and 
should be connected hydraulically to prefabricated drains. Permeable backfill material, such 
as open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation), wrapped with non-woven filter fabric 
should be placed around the weephole system.

design life per UFC 3-201-01 (Civil Engineering). Through discussions with the project 
team, we understand that the existing unpaved access road is primarily used by 
contractors doing work in the area. Therefore, we have assumed that daily traffic would 
consist of light pickup trucks, maintenance vehicles, and occasional large trucks. Based 
on our field observations, we have assumed that an average of 25 pickup trucks, 
10 maintenance vehicles, and two large trucks will drive up and down the reconstructed 
access road each day over the 25-year design life.

3.11 Rockfall Hazard Mitigation – Areas LR-1, 3, 5, & 6 
Based on the Government-provided information, Areas LR-1, 3, and 5 contain 
potential rockfall hazards. Our site reconnaissance program identified numerous loose 
boulders and relatively tall near-vertical rock outcrops that could generate potential 
rockfall hazards. Based on our evaluation of the existing site conditions, we believe a 
moderate to high potential for falling rock exists and that high priority should be assigned 
to the construction of rockfall protection systems in Areas LR-1, 3, and 5. 
For the project’s 35% Design Submittal, Geolabs identified potential rockfall 
hazards in “Area 6” (the upslope side of Upper Access Road between Adit 4 and Area 3). 
Numerous cobbles and boulders were noted in the relatively wide shoulder area during 
our field reconnaissance program. Based on our analyses, we believe that there may be

surface. Small trees, shrubbery, and grasses should be cut to expose the existing 
ground surfaces in preparation for rock slope scaling and boulder removal activity

3.11.2 Temporary Rockfall Protection 
We recommend the contractor install temporary rockfall protection measures along 
the base of the slope to protect the existing roadways and structures during the 
construction period. We understand the protection measures must permit vehicle 
access on the roadway and cannot encumber the normal operation of the gated 
entry point. Existing utilities, such as below-grade vaults, utility poles, and the 
existing concrete drainage swale should be protected from rockfall during the 
construction period. 
The use of steel roadway plates, movable flexible barrier systems, and other 
hardened protection measures should be deployed by the contractor to protect 
persons and property during the construction activity. The most critical time for 
deployment of the temporary protections will be during the rock slope scaling and 
boulder sweep removal efforts when large volumes of rock will be removed and 
dropped (scaled) to the roadway for collection and disposal.

3.11.4 Lower Slope Rock Scaling and Boulder Removal 
We recommend rock scaling of the cut slope faces and removal of some of the 
surface boulders from the lower slope within the limits of the rockfall impact barrier, 
draped wire mesh, and draped ring net installations. For Areas LR-1, 3, and 5, the 
scaling and boulder sweep area generally spans from the rockfall impact barrier 
alignment downslope toward the toe of the slope at the roadway. For Area 6, the 
scaling and boulder sweep area generally spans from about 10 feet above the 
planned top alignment of the draped wire mesh or draped ring net and extends 
downslope toward the toe of the cut slope at the roadway. Surface boulders should 
be removed, and unstable rock outcroppings should be scaled of loose rock and 
debris to prepare the ground for barrier alignment access and installation of the 
draped wire mesh and draped ring net. 
The boulder sweep effort consists of moving the existing boulders on the slope to 
the bottom of the slope for mass collection and disposal. Initiation of boulder 
movement could be by manual and/or mechanical methods to roll or push the rock 
downhill toward a designated collection point in a controlled manner. We envision 
the contractor will move the target boulders down slope to the top of the cut slope 
above the roadway. The rocks would then be dropped to the roadway level for 
mechanized collection and disposal. Temporary protection measures for 
infrastructure and personnel must be deployed prior to and during the boulder 
sweep/rock slope scaling efforts. Temporary protection measures may include a 
movable rockfall impact barrier with steel plates to protect existing bottom-of-slope 
infrastructure including the roadway, utilities, existing structures, and personnel. 
The methods of appropriate temporary protection are the responsibility of the 
contractor. In addition, we envision the need for traffic control through the work 
zone during the critical scaling and boulder sweep efforts conducted by the rock 
scaling crew.  
Rock slope scaling and boulder sweep activities consist of experienced crews 
employing manual labor and hand tools to remove loose rocks and precariously 
positioned rock fragments from the slope face. The scaling crews performing the 
rock slope scaling should be experienced in performing rock slope scaling by

should have experience or training in the use of emergency remote rescue 
techniques. 
The scaler foreman is an individual who is directly in charge of and responsible for 
the safety and work performed by a crew of three slope scalers. The scaler 
foreman should be actively engaged at the site location and should be engaged in 
actual slope scaling activities for at least 50 percent of the slope scaling crew time.

experience with a minimum of 2,000 hours of demonstrated experience performing 
slope scaling. Each scaler foreman should have completed the American Red 
Cross “basic first aid course” or equivalent, and should have experience or training 
in the use of emergency remote rescue techniques.

removing loose rock and materials from the slope face using a variety of hand tools 
at locations that require modified rock climbing techniques for the safe execution 
of the work. Each slope scaler should have a minimum of 2 years’ experience with

Prior to the start of the rock slope scaling, a series of eyebolt anchors may be 
installed along the top of the slope. The eyebolt anchors would be used as 
rappelling anchors. The eyebolt anchors may also be used to anchor other slope 
scaling apparatus, such as a grouser or other “spider” type platform. These 
platforms may be used to supplement the manual slope scaling to loosen/remove 
topographic overhangs and ledges. The scaling platforms are essentially dragged 
up and down the slope face to abrade and remove weakened ledges and 
overhangs that may be too difficult to be removed by hand and/or manual methods. 
The rock slope scaling will require complete closure of the roadway during the time 
that rock is being scaled or removed from the slope. We recommend all rock slope 
scaling work be conducted during daylight hours only. We envision that access for 
emergency vehicles should be provided at all times during the scaling work. 
We do not recommend the use of backhoe or other mechanized equipment for 
excavation and scaling at the project site. Due to the presence of interbedded 
layers of weak clinker materials, scaling with the use of a backhoe may be too 
aggressive to control the breakage of rock along existing fractures. In addition, 
scaling with the use of a backhoe may unnecessarily over-excavate clinker 
materials and cause potential new slope instability. Therefore, we believe the use 
of backhoe or other mechanized equipment to assist in the excavation and scaling 
work (to speed up the scaling work) should be prohibited. 
It should be noted that rock slope scaling work may be considered “hazardous” 
and should be classified as “high risk” work. Visibility is the most essential attribute 
to the successful performance of rock slope scaling by the rock slope scaling 
crews. Therefore, night work for rock slope scaling at the site should be

discouraged for crew safety reasons. In addition, the difficulty in maintaining proper

undesirable and virtually impossible. Furthermore, it should be noted that night 
work for rock slope scaling should be avoided because of potentially poor visibility 
and shadow conditions, which may reduce the ability of the rock slope scalers to 
properly assess the rock slope conditions and the presence of loose materials and 
precariously located rocks. 
3.11.5 Rockfall Impact Barrier 
Rockfall impact barriers are used worldwide as an effective rockfall mitigation and 
protection system. Rockfall impact barriers are commonly constructed on hillsides 
where the protection of downslope areas from various widespread and non-
specific sources of potential falling rock is necessary. Rockfall impact barriers are 
considered to be a viable rockfall protection measure where a specific and limited 
source of potential rockfall cannot be readily identified and stabilized, or where the 
source area may encompass disturbance-sensitive land that makes other 
mitigation measures impractical to implement. 
The rockfall impact barrier is a specialized barrier structure designed to flex and 
absorb the impact energy of falling boulders. The barrier system essentially 
consists of a series of steel posts linked by wire rope cables with steel ring nets 
and wire mesh material spanning between the posts. Lateral and upslope wire rope 
anchors that are installed in drilled holes and grouted in place with cement grout 
restrain the impact barrier posts. Rockfall impact barrier manufacturers fabricate 
the barrier components with varying capacities and dimensions based on the 
anticipated design load (rockfall impact energy) and probable rock bounce heights. 
Therefore, it is critical to carefully evaluate the existing site conditions and perform 
a statistical simulation of the rockfall activity to assist in the design of an 
appropriately sized rockfall impact barrier system. 
The impact barrier may be designed for varying load impacts that are estimated 
based on the existing site conditions such as boulder size, slope inclination and 
composition, computed rolling velocities, and possible boulder bounce heights 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 47 
Hawaii • California 
from rockfall impacts analyzed using the Colorado Rockfall Simulation Program 
(CRSP) computer software. 
The CRSP is a computer program that is a widely accepted engineering tool used 
by experienced personnel to estimate potential rockfall behavior by simulating 
rockfall activity based on input parameters that are assigned on a site-specific 
basis. The input parameters for this project were based on the observations and 
measurements collected in the field. The program provides a statistical evaluation 
of potential rockfall behavior based on the typical hillslope topographic profile and 
other parameters used to characterize the ground surface irregularities. 
Information obtained from the CRSP analysis includes the predicted falling rock 
velocity, bouncing height, kinetic energy, and roll-out distance. The output 
information is useful to assist in the site-specific design of various rockfall 
mitigation schemes such as rockfall impact barriers or other rockfall containment 
systems. 
In general, the CRSP computer program simulates various size boulders falling 
from specified slope geometries to provide statistical information for the anticipated 
rockfall velocity, rock bounce height, and rolling energy. Our rockfall simulation 
analysis was performed along multiple selected profiles to obtain basis of design 
parameters for an appropriate rockfall impact barrier system. Input variables to run 
the program (including probable rock block size, slope surface and vegetation 
conditions) were obtained during our site reconnaissance. In general, the following 
assumptions were used in our analyses.  
Size of boulders ranging from 2 to 6 feet in diameter 
Rockfall impact barrier designed based on 90 percent cumulative probability 
to retain the rockfall from the upper natural mountainside 
Based on the rockfall simulation results, we believe that construction of a 13-foot 
(4-meter) high, 369 foot-ton (1,000-kJ) capacity rockfall impact barrier across the 
lower slope above the roadway cut slope should be effective in capturing and 
retaining at least 90 percent of the simulated falling boulders from the upper portion 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 48 
Hawaii • California 
of the slope. The extents of the rockfall impact barriers at Area LR-1, Area 3, and 
Area 5 are presented on the Rockfall Hazard Mitigation Site Plans (Plates 6.1 to 
6.3). 
As previously mentioned, the rockfall impact barrier should be installed across the 
lower natural slope area just above the wire mesh and/or ring net drapery on the 
cut slope face. The purpose for the impact barrier is to intercept and retain potential 
high energy falling rock that could emanate from the upper natural slope. 
Based on our statistical analysis, 38 to 100 percent (with an average of 77 percent) 
of simulated rocks released from the hillside are shown to reach the bottom of the 
slope under the existing natural slope conditions without any rockfall mitigation 
improvement. By using a 369 foot-ton capacity barrier at a constructed height of 
about 13 feet above the ground surface at the lower slope location, over 90 percent 
of simulated rocks up to 6 feet in diameter should be captured and contained by 
the flexible rockfall impact barrier system. 
It should be noted that the generally accepted engineering standard of practice is 
to achieve at least 90 percent retention/control of potential falling rock hazards. 
Complete 100 percent control of potential falling rock hazards is often impractical 
and not cost effective to achieve. Rockfall is affected by many external factors 
including erosional decay, extreme weather, and other factors that generally 
cannot be controlled. However, through prudent study and application of available 
technology combined with high quality construction practices, the construction of 
rockfall mitigation improvements can effectively reduce the risk to an acceptable 
level. 
Rockfall impact barriers are designed to non-destructively contain multiple small 
quantity rockfall events without the need for barrier maintenance and repair. 
However, extreme rockfall events involving large quantities of rock material or very 
large boulders may require that the system be inspected for damage. Accumulated 
debris should be promptly removed to restore system effectiveness. As with any 
outdoor structure composed of steel components, deterioration and corrosion of 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 49 
Hawaii • California 
some system components over time may require the repair or replacement of worn 
components. In the event that very limited rockfall activity is experienced, the 
barrier system should be routinely inspected for signs of corrosion at a minimum 
of 5 to 10-year intervals. In Hawaii’s wet and humid environment, the lifespan of a 
galvanized steel rockfall barrier system generally is anticipated to range between 
about 20 and 30 years, after which time replacement of some system components 
may be required. A longer service life of about 50 years and beyond is possible 
with the use of special stainless steel. 
Three structural elements of rockfall impact barrier, including support post, lateral 
support cables, and upslope retaining cable would be subjected to the rockfall 
impact loads based on the design energy of the impact barrier. Therefore, the 
foundation should be designed to provide sufficient resistance to support these 
three structural elements. Based on the design energy of 369 foot-tons, the 
following loads were used for the rockfall impact barrier foundation design.

Based on the subsurface conditions anticipated, we recommend that a micropile 
foundation system be designed to support each barrier post due to the relatively 
high lateral load demand.  
In general, a micropile foundation system consists of a small diameter (usually less 
than 12 inches) drilled and grouted pile with steel reinforcing. The micropile 
foundation is typically constructed by drilling a borehole, placing reinforcing steel 
in the hole, and grouting the borehole. Micropiles are desirable because they can 
be installed readily in access restrictive environments and in numerous soil types 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 50 
Hawaii • California 
and ground conditions. In addition, installation of the micropiles generally causes 
minimal disturbance to adjacent soils and the environment. 
Based on our engineering analyses, we believe that a cased micropile system with 
a minimum grout bulb diameter of 5.5 inches may be used to support the barrier 
posts. Based on our analyses, we recommend installing four micropiles arranged 
in a 2-foot by 2-foot center-to-center pattern to provide for sufficient lateral load 
resistance for support of the barrier post. 
Based on the availability of equipment, we envision a cased micropile system with 
a minimum grout bulb diameter of 5.5 inches may be used. The load supporting 
capacity of the micropile foundation would be derived primarily from skin friction 
between the micropile bonded zone and the underlying basaltic materials. 
An allowable compressive load capacity of up to 22 kips may be used for the barrier 
post micropiles. The allowable compressive load capacities for the micropiles are 
for supporting dead-plus-live loads and may be increased by one third (⅓) for 
transient loads, such as wind or seismic forces. Due to the low lateral load 
resistance of a single micropile, we have assumed a micropile group consisting of 
four equally spaced micropiles for our design.  
We recommend providing permanent casing for the upper 5 feet of the micropiles. 
To achieve the allowable compressive load capacity with a factor of safety of 2, 
each micropile for would need to extend a minimum of about 10 feet into the 
underlying soil and/or rock material, or approximately 15 feet below the bottom of 
the pile cap (including the 5 feet of permanent casing). The permanent casing 
should have an outside diameter (OD) of at least 5.5 inches (same as the grout 
bulb size). The center reinforcing steel bar should consist of a No. 9 (Grade 75) 
reinforcing bar with a cross-section area of 1.0 square inch. The following table 
provides a summary of the micropile recommendations for ease of reference.
