To facilitate the micropile drilling and grouting, we recommend advancing the steel 
casing to the bottom of the micropile during the drilling operation. The steel casing 
may be withdrawn during the grouting operation while maintaining a minimum of 
5-foot grout head above the bottom of the casing at all times. The casing should 
be withdrawn above the design casing depth (minimum 3 feet) and plunged back 
to the design casing depth. 
In general, the performance of the micropiles will depend significantly upon the 
contractor’s method of installation and construction procedures. The load bearing 
capacities of micropiles depend on the friction between the pile and the 
surrounding ground. Therefore, the contractor should exercise care when drilling 
and placing concrete grout into the holes. 
Boulders and cobbles will likely be encountered during drilling for the micropiles; 
therefore, difficult drilling conditions should be expected. In addition, some basalt 
rock conditions may be encountered in the micropile drilled holes. The contractor 
will need to have the appropriate equipment and tools to drill through these types 
of obstructions (where encountered) in the subsurface. Appropriate measures will 
also be needed to avoid dislodging boulders and/or cobbles into the drilled hole 
during the drilling and installation process. The reinforcing bar should be placed in 
the center of the drilled hole using spacers to provide sufficient concrete grout 
placed around the reinforcing bar.

Due to the relatively small size of the drilled hole, we envision concrete grout 
placement may be conducted by tremie methods from the bottom up. Appropriate 
quality control of the concrete grout placement should be established to ensure 
good quality grout appearing on the top of the micropile. The concrete grout should 
be placed in a suitable manner to reduce the potential for segregation of the 
aggregates from the concrete grout mix. In addition, the concrete should be placed 
promptly after drilling to reduce the potential for softening of the sides of the drilled 
holes (saturation from drill water). 
We believe the wire rope anchor system may be designed to provide sufficient pull-
out resistance to support the lateral support cables and upslope retaining cables. 
The wire rope anchor system should consist of drilling a 6-inch diameter hole, 
installing minimum 1-inch diameter wire rope in the drilled hole, and grouting with 
non-shrink grout with a minimum unconfined compressive strength of 5,000 psi 
(28-day strength). We recommend that the wire rope anchors extend to a depth of 
about 15 feet below the ground surface.  
We recommend that pullout tests (proof tests) be performed on at least one 
selected production micropile on every other post column during construction to 
confirm the pull-out capacity. The pullout tests should consist of subjecting the 
micropiles to at least 150 percent of the allowable compressive load capacity and 
the maximum test load should be held for at least 10 minutes without appreciable 
movement. 
We also recommend that pullout tests (proof tests) be performed on a minimum of 
50 percent of the wire rope anchors during construction to confirm the pull-out 
capacity exceeding the above design loads. The pullout tests should consist of 
subjecting the wire rope anchors to at least 133 percent of the specified design 
loads and the maximum test load should be held for at least 10 minutes without 
appreciable movement. 
It is imperative that our representative or a qualified QC Specialist be present at 
the site to observe the drilling and installation of micropiles and wire rope anchors

3.11.6 Draped Wire Mesh and Draped Ring Nets 
The installation of draped wire mesh and draped ring nets covering the slope face 
serves to control the release of loose rock that may develop on the slope face in 
the years following the scaling work. In areas where relatively small rock blocks 
(about 3.5 feet in diameter or less) may dislodge from the existing cut slopes over 
time, we recommend a draped wire mesh system. In areas where larger rock 
blocks (about 3.5 feet to 6 feet in diameter) may dislodge from the existing cut 
slopes over time, we recommend a draped ring net system. Both systems should 
help retain dislodged rock blocks on the slopes, but more importantly, should allow 
for the controlled release of fallen rock toward the bases of the slopes at the 
roadway level. The locations of the draped wire mesh and draped ring net systems 
are presented on the Rockfall Hazard Mitigation Site Plans, Plates 6.1 to 6.3. 
The draped wire mesh and draped ring nets essentially contain broken rock 
material against the slope face as the fallen rock materials move downward by 
gravity. In this way, the rock fragments are deposited along the mesh/net outlet 
located at the base of the slope. A narrow containment area along the road 
shoulder should be provided to collect the accumulation of debris and allow for 
periodic mechanized cleanup. 
It is not the primary intent to closely pin the wire mesh and ring nets to the slope 
face as a retention system. The draped wire mesh and draped ring nets should 
provide positive rockfall control for the useful life of the drapery material. 
Upon completion of the rock slope scaling work, installation of the draped wire 
mesh and draped ring nets may proceed. Grouted wire rope anchors of about 
20 feet in depth and 6 inches in diameter should be installed into stiff 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 54 
Hawaii • California 
residual/saprolitic soil and/or weathered rock formation to achieve an allowable 
pull-out capacity of 25 tons (50 kips) and located about 5 to 10 feet beyond the 
brow of the rock cut slope. The grouted wire rope anchors generally are installed 
at about 25 feet on centers above the cut slope. Galvanized steel wire rope (1-inch 
diameter) should be strung continuously along the top of the slope through the 
lateral anchors at the corners and at the bends in the alignment of the steel wire 
rope, if applicable. The wire mesh and ring net fabric is attached and draped from 
the 0.75-inch cable ropes to cover the entire rock slope. In general, the 
manufacturer requires a lacing cable and interwoven cable between adjacent 
panels of the wire mesh and ring net panels.  
The rolls of wire mesh and ring nets are usually draped starting from the top of the 
slope. We envision the rolls of drapery material will be placed using a crane from 
the lower roadway. The drapery material is hung to within about 2 to 3 feet above 
the base level to allow for future removal of broken rock that may fall from behind 
the mesh and ring nets. The draped panels of mesh/ring nets are then tied together 
to complete the installation.  
Because of the subtropical climate and expected vegetation re-growth through the 
mesh, the draped wire mesh, draped ring nets, and hardware should be corrosion 
resistant. Therefore, we recommend the draped wire mesh, draped ring nets, and 
hardware be galvanized, as a minimum. The service life of the draped wire mesh 
and draped ring nets is estimated to be about 20 years if zinc-aluminum coating of 
the components is incorporated into the design. 
3.12 Shallow Foundations (Pipe Supports) 
We understand that pipe support footings will be required for the new drain lines 
along the slope between Upper and Lower Access Roads. Based on the anticipated 
subsurface conditions and structural loads, we believe that the pipe supports may be 
supported on shallow foundations. An allowable bearing pressure of up to 3,000 psf may 
be used for the design of spread and/or continuous footings bearing on the 
residual/saprolitic soils, weathered basalt rock formation, and/or compacted select 
granular fill needed to achieve the design finished grades. The bearing value 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021

W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 55 
Hawaii • California 
recommended is for dead-plus-live loads and may be increased by one-third (⅓) for 
transient loads, such as those caused by wind or seismic forces. 
Imported select granular fill should be compacted to at least 90 percent relative 
compaction to provide a relatively firm and smooth bearing surface prior to the placement 
of reinforcing steel and/or concrete. If soft and/or loose materials are encountered at the 
bottom of the footing excavations, they should be over-excavated to expose the 
underlying firm materials. The over-excavation may be backfilled with select granular 
materials compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction, or the bottom of 
footing may be extended down to bear directly on the underlying competent weathered 
basalt formation. 
In general, the bottom of footings should be embedded a minimum of 24 inches 
below the lowest adjacent finished grade. Footings located adjacent to slopes or on 
slopes should be embedded to a sufficient depth to provide a minimum horizontal setback 
distance of at least 6 feet measured from the outside edge of the footing to the face of 
the slope. Where the 6 feet horizontal setback distance is met, the minimum footing 
embedment may be ignored unless it is required by design for other considerations. 
Foundations located next to utility trenches or easements should be embedded below a 
45-degree imaginary plane extending upward from the bottom edge of the utility trench, 
or the footings should be extended to a depth as deep as the inverts of the utility lines. 
If the foundations are designed and constructed as recommended herein, we 
estimate total settlements of footings supported on the recompacted residual/saprolitic 
soils, weathered basalt rock formation, and/or compacted select granular fill to be on the 
order of about 1 inch or less. Differential settlements between adjacent footings supported 
on similar materials should be on the order of about 0.5 inches or less. 
Lateral loads acting on the structure may be resisted by a combination of frictional 
resistance between the base of the foundation and the bearing materials and by passive 
earth pressure developed against the near-vertical faces of the embedded portion of the 
footings. A coefficient of friction of 0.39 may be used for footings bearing on the 
recompacted residual/saprolitic soils, weathered basalt formation, or compacted select

granular fill. Resistance due to passive earth pressure may be estimated using an 
equivalent fluid pressure of 350 pounds per square foot per foot of depth. The passive 
earth pressure value assumes the concrete for the footings is cast neat against the 
relatively stiff residual/saprolitic soil, new compacted fills, and/or weathered basalt 
formation. Otherwise, the soils around the footings should be well compacted (minimum 
of 90 percent relative compaction). Unless covered by pavements or slabs, the passive 
pressure resistance in the upper 12 inches below the finished grade should be neglected. 
3.13 Below-Grade Retaining Structures 
Based on the information provided, we anticipate new drainage structures, 
generally consisting of drain manholes, box culverts, trench drains, and drain inlets, may 
be utilized for the drainage improvements planned at the project site. Based on our field 
exploration, we anticipate the new drainage structures will be constructed in the relatively 
stiff/dense residual/saprolitic soils and basalt formation encountered at the project site. 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered, the following general guidelines may 
be used for the design of the drainage structures at the project site. 
3.13.1 Drainage Structure Foundations 
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered, an allowable bearing pressure 
of up to 3,000 pounds per square foot (psf) may be utilized for design of the 
drainage structures bearing on the relatively stiff/dense residual/saprolitic soils 
and/or basalt formation at the project site. To provide uniform bearing support for 
the new drainage structures, we recommend providing a minimum 6-inch thick 
layer of open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) below the bottom of 
the drainage structures. For concrete box culverts, we recommend providing a 
minimum 6-inch thick layer of aggregate subbase material compacted to a 
minimum of 90 percent relative compaction in lieu of the 6-inch thick layer of open-
graded gravel. We anticipate the drain manhole, catch basin, and drain inlet 
structures will likely be pre-cast. 
Foundation subgrades should be recompacted to a firm and unyielding surface 
prior to the placement of the open-graded gravel and/or aggregate subbase 
material. Soft and/or loose materials encountered at the bottom of the excavations

should be over-excavated to expose the underlying firm materials or a maximum 
of 2 feet below the bottom of the footing. The over-excavation should be backfilled 
with select granular fill materials compacted to a minimum of 90 percent relative 
compaction.  
Based on our engineering analyses, we believe that the net increase in bearing 
pressure on the underlying stiff/dense residual/saprolitic soils and/or basalt 
formation resulting from the drainage structure construction would be minimal. 
Settlements on the order of 0.5 inches or less are anticipated for the drainage 
structures.

force should be assumed to act through the mid-height of the wall. An appropriately 
reduced factor of safety may be used when dynamic lateral earth forces are 
accounted for in the design of the below-grade structure walls.  
3.13.3 Backfilling Drainage Structures 
The drainage structure excavations will need to be properly backfilled to reduce 
the potential for subsidence at the ground surface. The excavated on-site soils or 
imported fill materials that are free of vegetation, deleterious materials, and clay 
lumps and rock fragments greater than 3 inches in maximum dimension may be 
used as backfill up to the finished subgrades.  
Drainage structure backfills should be moisture-conditioned to at least 2 percent 
above the optimum moisture content, placed in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches 
in loose thickness, and compacted to at least 90 percent relative compaction. 
Where drainage structures are located below pavement areas, the upper 3 feet of

3.14 Site Grading 
We anticipate grading work will primarily consist of excavations for segmental 
retaining walls and preparing segmental retaining wall and shallow foundation subgrades; 
cutting and filling Areas SS-1 and SS-2; re-grading the eroded soil slope above the 
Area RR-1 water tanks; excavating for underground drainage structures and utility 
trenches and subsequent backfilling; and preparing pavement subgrades. Items of site 
grading that are addressed in the subsequent subsections include the following: 
1. 
Site Preparation 
2. 
Fills and Backfills 
3. 
Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements 
4. 
Cut and Fill Slopes 
5. 
Excavations 
A Geolabs representative or a qualified QC Specialist should monitor site grading 
operations to observe whether undesirable materials are encountered during the 
excavation and scarification process and to confirm whether the exposed soil and/or rock 
conditions are similar to those assumed in this report. 
3.14.1 Site Preparation 
At the on-set of earthwork, areas within the contract grading limits should be 
thoroughly cleared and grubbed. Vegetation, debris, deleterious materials, existing 
structures to be demolished, and other unsuitable materials should be removed 
and disposed of properly off-site to reduce the potential for contamination of the 
excavated materials. 
Soft and yielding areas encountered during clearing and grubbing below areas 
designated to receive fill and/or future improvements should be over-excavated to 
expose firm natural material, and the resulting excavation should be backfilled with 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 60 
Hawaii • California 
well-compacted fill. The excavated soft soils should not be re-used as fill materials 
and should be disposed of properly off-site. 
In general, the subgrades in cut areas and areas designated to receive fills should 
be scarified to a depth of about 8 inches, moisture-conditioned to at least 2 percent 
above the optimum moisture content, and recompacted to a minimum of 
90 percent relative compaction. The compaction requirements for finished 
subgrades subjected to vehicular traffic should be increased to a minimum of 
95 percent relative compaction. Relative compaction refers to the in-place dry 
density of soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density as 
determined by ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage 
by dry weight) corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
Where basalt formation is exposed, the subgrades in cut areas and areas 
designated to receive fills should be proof-rolled with a large vibratory drum roller 
(minimum 10 tons static weight) for a minimum of eight passes to help detect and 
collapse near-surface cavities and/or voids. The vibratory drum roller should be 
operated at a speed of about 300 feet per minute. 
Where shrinkage cracks are observed after the subgrade compaction, we 
recommend preparing the subgrade soil again as described above. Saturation and 
subsequent yielding of the exposed subgrade due to inclement weather and poor 
drainage may require over-excavating the soft areas and replacing these areas 
with compacted fill. A Geolabs field representative or a qualified QC Specialist 
should evaluate the need for over-excavation due to soft subgrade soil conditions. 
3.14.2 Fills and Backfills 
In general, the excavated on-site materials should be suitable for use as general 
fill materials provided that the maximum particle size is less than 3 inches in largest 
dimension. The on-site soils and basalt rock materials generated from the 
excavations may be used as a source of general fill or backfill materials provided 
that they are screened of the over-sized materials and/or processed to meet the 
above gradation requirements (less than 3 inches in largest dimension).

SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 8128-00 & 10

Imported materials to be used as select granular fill should be non-expansive 
granular materials, such as crushed coral or basalt. The select granular fill should 
be well graded from coarse to fine with no particles larger than 3 inches in largest 
dimension. The material should have a California Bearing Ratio (CBR) value of 20 
or higher, and a swell potential of 1 percent or less when tested in accordance with 
ASTM D1883. The material also should contain between 10 and 30 percent 
particles passing the No. 200 sieve. An accredited testing laboratory should test 
imported fill materials a minimum of 10 days prior to being transported to the 
project site for the intended use.  
3.14.3 Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements 
Fill materials consisting of the on-site soils should be moisture-conditioned to at 
least 2 percent above the optimum moisture content, placed in level lifts not 
exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent 
relative compaction. Fill materials consisting of the excavated basalt rock materials 
should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture, placed in level lifts 
not exceeding 8 inches in loose thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 
90 percent relative compaction. Relative compaction refers to the in-place dry 
density of soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same 
soil established in accordance with ASTM D1557. Optimum moisture is the water 
content (percentage by weight) corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
Imported fill materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum 
moisture content, placed in level lifts of about 8 inches in loose thickness, and 
compacted to a minimum of 90 or 95 percent relative compaction as appropriate. 
Aggregate subbase materials should be moisture-conditioned to above the 
optimum moisture content, placed in level lifts not exceeding 8 inches in loose 
thickness, and compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. 
Compaction should be accomplished by sheepsfoot rollers or other types of 
acceptable compaction equipment. Water tamping, jetting, or ponding should not 
be allowed to compact the fills. Where compaction is less than required, additional

GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 62

compactive effort should be applied with adjustment of moisture content, as 
necessary, to obtain the specified compaction. 
3.14.4 Cut and Fill Slopes 
We envision cut slopes at the project site will generally expose the relatively 
stiff/dense residual/saprolitic soils and/or basalt rock formation encountered in our 
borings. In general, cut slopes and permanent fill slopes may be designed with a 
slope inclination of 2H:1V or flatter. Fills placed on slopes steeper than 5H:1V 
should be keyed and benched into the existing slope to provide stability of the new 
fill against sliding. 
Construction of earth berms, interceptor ditches, and the use of geotextile fabrics 
over the fill slope face should be considered to reduce the potential for significant 
erosion, thus enhancing the long-term stability of the fill slopes. Appropriate slope 
planting or other erosion control measures to reduce the potential for significant 
erosion of the exposed slopes (including a permanent irrigation system) should be 
implemented as soon as possible after the finished slope faces are completed. 
3.14.5 Excavation 
Our field exploration indicated that the majority of the project site may be underlain 
by medium hard to hard basalt rock formation at relatively shallow depths. 
Therefore, we anticipate that some difficult excavation conditions may arise during 
construction that will require the use of hoerams or chipping. It should be noted 
that laboratory compression testing of the basalt formation samples yielded 
unconfined compressive strengths of up to about 24,360 pounds per square inch 
(psi).  
The above discussions regarding the rippability of the materials are based on our 
field and laboratory data from the drilled borings. We recommend encouraging 
contractors to examine the site conditions and the subsurface data to make their 
own reasonable and prudent interpretation.

3.15 Utility Trenches 
We anticipate that underground utility connections will be required for the project. 
In general, good construction practices should be utilized for the installation and 
backfilling of the trenches for the new utilities. The contractor should determine the 
method and equipment to be used for trench excavation, subject to practical limits and 
safety considerations. In addition, the excavations should comply with the applicable 
federal, state, and local safety requirements. The contractor should be responsible for 
trench shoring design and installation. 
In general, we recommend providing granular bedding consisting of 6 inches of 
open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 gradation) under the pipes for uniform support. 
Free-draining granular materials, such as open-graded gravel (ASTM C33, No. 67 
gradation), should also be used for the initial trench backfill up to about 12 inches above 
the pipes to provide adequate support around the pipes. It is critical to use this 
free-draining material to reduce the potential for formation of voids below the haunches 
of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the pipes. Improper trench 
backfill could result in backfill settlement and pipe damage. 
The upper portion of the trench backfill from the level 12 inches above the pipes to 
the top of the subgrade or finished grade may consist of on-site soils or select granular 
fill material. Backfill material consisting of the on-site residual and/or saprolitic soils should 
be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture content, placed in maximum 
8-inch level loose lifts, and mechanically compacted to at least 90 percent relative 
compaction. Backfill material consisting of the excavated basalt rock material or imported 
select granular fill should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture 
content, placed in maximum 8-inch level loose lifts, and mechanically compacted to at 
least 90 percent relative compaction. In areas where trenches will be in paved areas, the 
upper 3 feet of the trench backfill below the pavement finished grade should be 
compacted to no less than 95 percent relative compaction. Mechanical compaction 
equipment should be used to compact the backfill materials. Compaction efforts by water 
tamping, jetting, or ponding should not be allowed.

Preliminary and final drawings and specifications for the project should be

the geotechnical recommendations provided herein. If this review is not made, Geolabs 
cannot be held responsible for misinterpretation of our recommendations.

In addition, a Geolabs representative or QC Specialist should monitor the other 
aspects of earthwork construction to observe compliance with the intent of the design 
concepts, specifications, or recommendations and to expedite suggestions for design 
changes that may be required in the event that subsurface conditions differ from those 
anticipated at the time this report was prepared. The recommendations provided in this 
report are contingent upon such observations. 
If the actual exposed subsurface conditions encountered during construction are 
different from those assumed or considered in this report, then appropriate modifications 
to the design should be made.

SECTION 4.  LIMITATIONS 
The analyses and recommendations presented in this report are based, in part, 
upon information obtained from our test borings, bulk samples, DCP testing, and 
laboratory test data. Variations of the subsurface conditions between and beyond the field 
data points may occur and the nature and extent of these variations may not become 
evident until construction is underway. If variations then appear evident, it will be 
necessary to re-evaluate the recommendations presented herein. 
The field boring locations indicated herein are approximate, having been surveyed 
by ControlPoint Surveying, Inc. Elevations of the borings were estimated from spot 
elevations and contours shown on the Topographic Survey Map created by ControlPoint 
Surveying, Inc. dated September 14, 2020. The field boring locations and elevations 
should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification breaks shown on the graphic representations of the borings 
depict the approximate boundaries between soil types and, as such, may denote a 
gradual transition. Groundwater was not encountered in the drilled borings at the time of 
our field exploration. However, it should be noted that groundwater fluctuations may occur 
due to rainfall, temperature, perched groundwater, and other factors. This data has been 
reviewed and interpretations made in the formulation of this report. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of Belt Collins Hawaii LLC and 
their project consultants for specific application to the design of the Repair Roads and 
Erosion Control (FY21 Special Project RM 17-1635) project at Red Hill in accordance with 
generally accepted geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is 
expressed or implied. 
This report has been prepared solely for the purpose of assisting the design 
engineers in the design of the project. Therefore, this report may not contain sufficient 
data, or the proper information, to serve as a basis for detailed construction cost 
estimates.  
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
SECTION 4. LIMITATIONS 
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 66 
Hawaii • California 
The owner/client should be aware that unanticipated soil conditions are commonly 
encountered. Unforeseen subsurface conditions, such as soft deposits, hard layers, or 
cavities may occur in localized areas and may require additional probing or corrections in 
the field (which may result in construction delays) to attain a properly constructed project. 
Therefore, a sufficient contingency fund is recommended to accommodate these possible 
extra costs. 
This geotechnical engineering exploration conducted at the project site was not 
intended to investigate the potential for presence of hazardous materials existing at the 
project site. It should be noted that the equipment, techniques, and personnel used to 
conduct a geo-environmental exploration differ substantially from those applied in 
geotechnical engineering. 
END OF LIMITATIONS

APPENDIX A 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
OCTOBER 2021        Page A-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions at the project site by drilling and sampling 
36 borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 30 and P-1 through P-6, extending to 
depths of about 5 to 42 feet below the existing ground surface. In addition, nineteen bulk 
soil samples, designated as Bulk-1 through Bulk-19, were obtained to evaluate the 
pavement support characteristics of the near-surface soils. The approximate boring 
locations are shown on the Overall Site Plan (Plate 3) and the individual Site Plans 
(Plates 4.1 through 4.4). The borings were drilled using a truck-mounted drill rig equipped 
with continuous flight augers and coring tools.  
Our field engineer classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations 
on a near-continuous (full-time) basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually 
and by testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with 
ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes 
(Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. 
Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with ASTM D2487 are described 
on the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic representations of the materials 
encountered are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1.1 through A-36. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained in general accordance with 
ASTM D3550, Ring-Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD Modified 
California sampler with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples 
were obtained from the drilled borings in general accordance with ASTM D1586, 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. The 
penetration resistance shown on the Logs of Borings indicates the number of blows 
required for the specific sampler type used. The blow counts may need to be factored to 
obtain the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) blow counts. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques in general accordance with ASTM D2113, 
Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling method that 
uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the hollow core of 
the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. Rock cores were 
described in general accordance with the Rock Description System as shown on the Rock 
Log Legend, Plate A-0.3. The Rock Description System is based on the publication 
“Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” 
by the International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977). 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
Appendix A 
Field Exploration 
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
OCTOBER 2021        Page A-2 
Hawaii • California 
Recovery (REC) is used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the relative 
quality of rock masses. Recovery is defined as the actual length of material recovered 
from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. For example, if 3.7 feet of 
material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery would be 74 percent and 
would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run in rock that is 
sound material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting any 
drilling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is recovered from a 
5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on the Logs 
of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the relative quality 
of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks and 
Minerals." 
Rock Quality 
RQD 
(%) 
Very Poor 
0 – 25 
Poor 
25 – 50 
Fair 
50 – 75 
Good 
75 – 90 
Excellent 
90 – 100 
The rippability (or excavation characteristics) of a rock mass is a function of the 
relative hardness of the rock, its relative quality, brittleness, and fissile characteristics. 
Dense basalt formations with high RQD values would be very difficult to rip and would 
probably require more arduous methods of excavation.

PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., FAT CLAY,
ELASTIC SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY).
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SILTY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.

with traces of: <5%
accessory descriptions are lower cased and follow the
Primary and Secondary Constituents
(i.e., SILTY GRAVEL with a little sand)

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite
CONGLOMERATE

OCTOBER 2021        Page B-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Dynamic Cone Penetration Tests 
We explored the subsurface conditions at Areas SS-1 and SS-2 and along Upper 
Access Road by performing Dynamic Cone Penetration (DCP) tests at twenty-seven 
locations extending to depths of about 0.5 to 17 feet below the existing ground surface. 
The approximate DCP locations are shown on the Overall Site Plan (Plate 3) and the 
individual Site Plans (Plates 3.1 through 3.4). Field logs of the Dynamic Cone Penetration 
tests are presented on Plates B-1.00 through B-27.06. 
The procedures used in our DCP tests are in general accordance with 
ASTM D6951, Standard Test Method for Use of the Dynamic Cone Penetrometer in 
Shallow Pavement Applications. The DCP apparatus consists of a 5/8-inch diameter steel 
rod with a 60-degree conical tip. The rod is topped with an anvil that is connected to a 
second steel rod. This rod is used as a guide to allow a 17.6-lb hammer to be repeatedly 
raised and dropped from a height of 22.6 inches. The connection between the two rods 
consists of an anvil to allow for quick connections between the rods and for efficient 
energy transfer from the falling weight to the penetrating rod. 
Once the test apparatus is assembled, the DCP is placed at the test location and 
the initial penetration of the rod is recorded to provide a zeroing scale. While holding the 
rod vertically, the weight is raised to the top of the rod (22.6 inches above the anvil) and 
dropped. The penetration of the rod is measured after each drop. 
In the DCP tests conducted by Geolabs, the same equipment described above 
was used to record the depth of penetration per hammer drop as shown in the field logs 
provided in Appendix B.

APPENDIX  C 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
OCTOBER 2021       Page C-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   C 
Laboratory Tests 
Moisture Content (ASTM D2216) and Unit Weight (ASTM D2937) determinations 
were performed on selected samples as an aid in the classification and evaluation of soil 
properties. The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths. 
Nineteen Atterberg Limits tests (ASTM D4318) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the liquid and plastic limits. The test results are summarized on the 
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentations of the test 
results are provided on Plate C-1 and C-2. 
Eight Sieve Analysis tests (ASTM D6913) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the soils and to aid in soil 
classification. Graphic presentations of the grain size distributions are provided on 
Plates C-3 and C-4. 
Fourteen One-Inch Ring Swell tests were performed on remolded samples to 
evaluate the swelling potential of the on-site soils. The test results are summarized on 
Plate C-5. 
To evaluate the unconfined compressive strength of the on-site clayey soils, 
one unconfined compression test was performed on a selected soil sample in accordance 
with ASTM D2166. The test result is shown on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depth. The individual stress-strain curve of the unconfined compression test is 
presented on Plate C-6. 
Three direct shear tests (ASTM D3080) were performed on selected samples to 
evaluate the shear strength characteristics of the materials tested. Results of the direct 
shear tests are presented on Plates C-7 through C-9. 
To evaluate the long-term shear strengths of the clayey soils, one 
Consolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression test was performed on a selected, 
relatively undisturbed soil sample in accordance with ASTM D4767. The test results and 
stress-strain curves are presented on Plates C-10.1 through C-10.3. 
One hundred thirteen (113) unconfined compression tests (ASTM D7012 Method 
C) were performed on selected rock cores to evaluate their uniaxial compressive strength 
of the rock cores obtained. Results of the unconfined compression tests are presented 
on Plates C-11 through C-15. 
Sixteen Modified Proctor compaction tests (ASTM D1557) were performed on bulk 
samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the dry density and moisture content 
relationships. The test results are presented on Plates C-16 through C-31. 
DRAFT #3 Geotech Eng. Rpt. - Final Design Submittal - Oct. 2021
Appendix C 
Laboratory Tests 
W.O. 8128-00 & 10 
GEOLABS, INC. 
OCTOBER 2021       Page C-2 
Hawaii • California 
Nineteen laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were performed 
on bulk samples of the near-surface soils to evaluate the pavement support 
characteristics of the soils. The test results are presented on Plates C-32 through C-50.

NOTE:

Remolded

They were air-dried overnight and then saturated for 24 hours under a surcharge pressure of 55 psf.
